# Angular Essential Training

> Angular helps developers and programmers build and maintain complex applications. The popular, widely used JavaScript platform gives you a solid foundation of web-based functionality, letting you take care of the design and implementation details as you go.In this course, instructor Derek Peruo introduces you to the essentials of this incredible platform, including some of its most powerful featur

#### What you should know
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To get the most out of this course, you should have a good idea of what TypeScript is and how it works.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=5)** Because we write Angular code using TypeScript.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=8)** You should also know how to write HTML and CSS because we use those languages when writing Angular components.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=15)** It's a good idea to know how to use npm because Angular uses Node and npm under the hood to help streamline your project's workflow.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=22)** You can always check out our course library for more info on TypeScript, NTML, CSS, and Node.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=27)** Angular comes with full support for all modern browsers and operating systems.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=31)** I'll use Google Chrome and Mac OS during this course because that's what I'm most comfortable with.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=36)** But you can use any browser and operating system that works well for you.

#### Using the example code and codespaces

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Speaker] In this course, we'll look at all the essential skills and techniques you need when building an angular application at any size.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=6)** I organized this course by topic, so feel free to skip around and watch the content that's most relevant for you or watch all the way through start to finish for a full rundown of all the essential skills and techniques.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=18)** I included a downloadable file with links to more information about some of the topics we'll cover.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=24)** I sorted the links by chapter and video to make it easy to find what you're looking for.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=29)** Whenever possible, I linked to the official documentation for each technique or tool.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=33)** Official documentation is the best source for up-to-date info on everything covered in this course.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=39)** You can also follow along using the example code which we host on GitHub at this URL.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=45)** There's only one branch in this repo, the main branch and all the videos in this course use the same code.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=51)** Feel free to make new branches to explore on your own.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=54)** While you could clone the repo to your local machine, I recommend using GitHub's Codespaces instead, if you can.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=62)** Codespaces are secure development environments hosted in the cloud on GitHub's servers.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=67)** They give you and your team all the power of Visual Studio Code but without downloading anything to your local machine.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=73)** This makes it easy to control access, share settings and guarantees that everyone uses the same environment when writing and running code.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=82)** As of this recording, you'll need a GitHub account to use Codespaces.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=86)** After you log in, make sure you're on the main page for the repo and click this green dropdown menu where it says Code.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=93)** Inside the menu, you'll have options to clone the repo to your local machine or use a codespace.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=101)** You can click this plus sign here to make a new Codespace or click the name of an existing Codespace if you have one.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=109)** Or you might see a big green button that says Create Codespace.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=112)** Click it to make a new Codespace.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=115)** I'll click on my existing Codespace to launch it.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=119)** Any way you do it, GitHub will launch the environment and get everything set up.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=123)** It may take a moment, so please be patient.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=127)** Once everything is up and running, we're ready to write and execute code.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=133)** By default, the interface for the Codespace looks and feels very much like Visual Studio Code.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=140)** If you're familiar with VS code, you'll feel right at home inside a codespace.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=145)** Here are three things to keep in mind when working with Codespaces.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=149)** First, GitHub hosts all Codespaces on their secure remote servers.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=153)** This means you won't have access to your local tools or configurations unless you upload them to GitHub.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=159)** Check out GitHub's official documentation for how to do that.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=162)** Second, when launching a dev server using ng Serve, you must set the host option to 0.0.0.0.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=171)** You should also disable the host check.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=173)** We need to do this because of how Codespace's virtual machines forward ports from inside the container to your browser.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=181)** I updated the npm start command to do this for you.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=183)** Let me show you what that looks like.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=186)** I'll open up a new terminal here in our Codespace, type npm start, and then hit enter.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=194)** Angular CLI will compile the code and launch the server.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=199)** This alert here in the lower right hand corner tells me I have a successful connection to the dev server.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=205)** I can click this blue button to launch a new browser tab or I can command click this URL here to do the same thing.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=212)** Either way, our project opens in a new tab so we can see our changes in real time when we write our code.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=220)** Last thing to keep in mind is that GitHub puts limits on the size, speed and number of code spaces you can have running at one time.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=228)** The limits vary based on the type of account you have.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=231)** For many projects, especially public open source projects, the free monthly quota available with all accounts is plenty of computing power to get the job done.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=240)** Consider upgrading to a paid plan.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=242)** If you need Codespaces for private repos or you're working with a large number of team members.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-the-example-code-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=246)** Check out the exercise files for links to more info.

#### Application overview
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] This is the example app we'll work with in this course.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=3)** It's an online gaming platform called Gem Finder.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=7)** On launch, the app takes us to the all players page.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=10)** From here, we can search for users by name.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=13)** For example, I'll start typing Davis.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=17)** The list will update automatically to filter the list based on the search term.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=22)** We can clear the list using this button here.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=24)** The lightning bolt next to some of the names tell us which players are online right now.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=30)** Clicking on any of the names brings up the profile page for that player.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=34)** The game tracks two pieces of info for each player, their current score and how many gems they collected.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=41)** We can also see the player's unique ID here.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=44)** Clicking the all players tab on the left here takes us back to the all players page.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=50)** Clicking the center tab here takes us to the leader boards page.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=55)** The game has two leader boards.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=56)** The first shows the top five highest scores and the second shows the top five players who collected the most gems.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=66)** We can click on any name here and it takes us to the profile for that user.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=72)** The URL and page title updates when we navigate to any page in the game.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=77)** This makes it easy to bookmark specific pages and navigate using the browser's history.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=83)** Clicking this tab here on the right takes us to the message inbox.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=88)** Clicking the little close icon to the right of each message deletes that message from the list and updates the unread messages count in the app and in the browser tab.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=101)** Let's take a look at the code.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=103)** I opened our project here in a GitHub code space.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=106)** I talk about why we use code spaces earlier in this course.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=108)** For now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=114)** At the root of the project folder are all our configuration files.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=118)** This is where we store settings for Angular, NPM, the TypeScript Compiler, and our code editor.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=124)** We'll talk more about some of these files later in this course.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=128)** The source folder is where we keep all our custom application code.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=132)** I'll go to the source folder and click on index.html to open that file.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=137)** This file is a pretty standard HTML file.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=140)** We load the actual Angular application using this app-root element on line 22.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=147)** Bootstrap is a popular open source toolkit that helps make the CSS in our project look a little nicer.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=153)** Bootstrap works very well with Angular applications but it's not required.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=157)** Angular will work just fine without Bootstrap.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=160)** The app-root element is a custom selector Angular looks for when loading our application.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=166)** We could call this element anything we like but it's usually called app-root.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=171)** Next, I'll go to the source folder and click on main.ts to open the file.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=176)** This file tells Angular which module to load first on application launch.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=181)** We'll talk more about modules in the bootstrap process later in this course.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=185)** Let's also take a look at the styles.css file.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=190)** This file is our main style sheet for the project.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=193)** All pages and components have access to styles defined in this file.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=198)** We're using it in this project to override Bootstraps defaults with our custom fonts and colors, but you can put any valid CSS in here.

> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=213)** This file has all the static data that represents the players in our app.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=218)** We're using mock data in this course to avoid the need for a real remote server.

> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=222)** The same goes for this messages.json file in the same folder it has all the static data that represents unread messages.

> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=231)** Finally, there's the app folder.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=235)** This is where most of our custom application code lives.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=239)** You'll spend most of your time in this folder when building an Angular project.

> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=243)** I wrote the code for this example app to look and feel like code you might see when working on a real project.

> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/application-overview?u=76281980&t=255)** The topics we'll cover in this course are still valid and will work no matter what folder structure you use.

### 1. Overview of Angular

#### History and ownership
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=0)** - Every project starts because there's a problem that needs to be solved.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=4)** Angular is no different.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=6)** Knowing the history and philosophy of Angular may help you understand why we write Angular code in certain ways.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=12)** Angular started life at Google as a side project to help employees build internal web apps.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=18)** What made Angular unique was its focus on extending HTML markup with custom attributes and dynamic content, instead of using JavaScript to generate the HTML at runtime.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=29)** This was a revolutionary way of writing code, and it allowed developers to keep templates separate from controllers.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=36)** Google released version one of Angular to the public back in 2010, and the community loved it.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=42)** I've worked with Angular for over a decade, and I've seen firsthand how it's matured into a stable platform with full support for everything you need to build web applications at scale.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=52)** As of this recording, the Angular framework still has a strong focus on keeping HTML and JavaScript separate.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=58)** I like this separation of concerns, and it's one of the reasons Angular is my framework of choice for new projects.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/history-and-ownership?u=76281980&t=65)** Google's full-time Angular team maintains the framework with the help of a large community of open-source contributors, and Google still currently owns the copyright.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** html (3)
> **Code Keywords:** public (1)
> **Speakers:** - every (1)

#### Release and support cycles
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=0)** - No matter how big your project is, the JavaScript framework you used must be stable and secure.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=6)** The more stable the framework, the more you and your team can plan long-term releases for your project.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=11)** This is why it's vital, to know the release and support cycles, for the frameworks you might use, including Angular.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=17)** Angular uses semantic versioning, to track changes, and releases.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=22)** Semantic versioning, SEM VER for short, is both a strategy and a naming convention.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=27)** It helps you and everyone else know, exactly what to expect from each new release.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=32)** This makes it easier to plan upgrades in your project.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=35)** There are always three types of releases when using SEM VER.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=39)** Major releases, minor releases, and patch releases, and while there are official definitions, for each type of release, Angular has its own unique flavor.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=47)** In Angular, major releases contain big new features.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=51)** This type of release is sometimes called a breaking change, because you may need to learn new APIs, refactor your code or update config files.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=60)** Angular publishes a new major release every six months, and they usually include an upgrade path, for previous versions.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=67)** Minor releases contain smaller, new features, that are backwards compatible with previous versions.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=73)** There's usually no need to refactor code, when upgrading to a new minor release.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=77)** Patch releases are usually low risk fixes, for bugs and security.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=81)** It's a good idea to upgrade, to the latest patch release as soon as you can.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=86)** Angular publishes one to three minor releases, for each major release, but there's no fixed release dates.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=92)** Angular publishes a new patch release about once per week.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=96)** The Angular team supports each new major release, for 18 months.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=100)** The first six months are considered active support, with both minor and patch releases, and the next 12 months are considered long-term support, with only patch releases.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=109)** One more thing to keep in mind.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=111)** The Angular team usually releases a new version, of all their NPM packages, whenever they release a new version of the core framework.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=118)** This helps make sure that all the packages work together, and makes it easy to upgrade all of your Angular dependencies at once.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/release-and-support-cycles?u=76281980&t=124)** Using the NG update command, from Angular's official CLI tool.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** sem (2), ver (2), npm (1), cli (1)
> **CLI Commands:** npm (1), make (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)
> **Speakers:** - no (1)

#### Angular licensing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=0)** - A software license is a legal document that governs where and how you can use a piece of code owned by someone else.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=7)** Some licenses have a lot of restrictions that require payment to use the software.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=12)** Other licenses are more permissive.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=14)** And some developers release their software into the public domain, which has no restrictions at all.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=19)** The type of license that comes with your JavaScript framework matters.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=22)** This is especially true if you're building an application that includes proprietary code or that end users will pay for.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=29)** Some licenses won't let you do that.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=31)** Lucky for us, as of this recording, Angular is released under a very permissive license called the MIT license.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=39)** This license started back in the late 1980s at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, hence the name, MIT.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=46)** Some places call it the Expat license or the X11 license, but the terms of use are mostly the same.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=52)** It's one of the most popular licenses out there for open-source projects, and it's also one of the shortest, at only about 165 words.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=60)** The license gives you and your team the freedom to use the software free of charge for whatever you want, as long as you include the copyright notice and license info.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=70)** The MIT license is compatible with the GNU General Public License, as well as Debian's free software guidelines.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=76)** And it's approved by the Free Software Foundation and the Open Source Initiative.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=81)** Depending on other software you use in your project, you may need this extra compatibility and approval.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=86)** The only downside of such a permissive license might be its lack of warranty.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=90)** Software released under the MIT license is provided as is, and the copyright holders cannot be held liable if something goes wrong when you use the software.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=100)** That might sound scary, but many developers, including me, feel the benefits of using software available under an MIT license outweigh its lack of warranty.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=109)** While every project is unique, I've never had an issue using MIT license software in my work.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=114)** Your needs might be different, and you may need to use software that comes with a warranty.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-licensing?u=76281980&t=118)** I recommend you speak with your legal team to confirm if the MIT license will work for your specific needs.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** mit (7), x11 (1), gnu (1)
> **Code Keywords:** public (2), else. (1), require (1), for. (1), let (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - a (1)

#### Out of the box
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=0)** - The Angular framework was built to solve specific types of problems when it comes to web applications.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=5)** This means a fresh install comes with its own benefits and limitations.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=10)** Angular was originally designed to build single-page applications, or SPAs, for short.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=15)** SPAs, at their core. are just websites where the content updates dynamically without the need to refresh the browser.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=22)** This makes the website feel more like a native app experience.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=26)** What made Angular unique was its focus on extending HTML Markup, with custom attributes and dynamic content.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=32)** This was a revolutionary way of writing code, and it allowed developers to keep templates separate from controllers.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=39)** With many frameworks, developers had to embed the HTML templates inside JavaScript files and manipulate the DOM directly.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=47)** Some modern frameworks still embrace this strategy, and there's nothing wrong with it.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=50)** It's just that Angular does things differently.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=53)** Today, Angular is still great for single-page applications, but the ecosystem's evolved.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=59)** Now, there's reusable components, standard libraries, and routing.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=63)** This is different from other modern frameworks that focus on just building complex user interfaces or maximizing compatibility with other libraries and frameworks.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=72)** The official website positions Angular as a platform, not just a framework.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/out-of-the-box?u=76281980&t=76)** And I think this description is accurate because you can build any type of application at scale, right out of the box.

>
> **Env Vars:** html (2), dom (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (1)
> **Speakers:** - the (1)

#### Workflow and tooling
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=0)** - As of this recording, Angular comes with its own ecosystem of developer tools and workflows.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=5)** Many Angular projects use this ecosystem, and all Angular developers should know how it works.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=11)** Angular CLI is the official command line tool for Angular projects.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=15)** It's open source and release under the MIT License.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=18)** The Angular team releases a new major version of the CLI tool, when they release a new major version of the framework itself.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=25)** They release minor and patch versions as needed, independent of the framework.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=30)** The major version of the CLI tool usually has the same number as the framework it works with.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=34)** So version 15 of the CLI tool works with version 15 of the framework and so on.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=40)** This makes it easy to upgrade all of your project's dependencies at once when needed.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=45)** Angular's command line tool is called ng.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=47)** It's a robust utility you can use to do all sorts of things in your project.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=51)** There are a lot of commands but here are five I use all the time in my work.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=56)** ng build to compile code and output files into a bill directory, ng serve to compile code, launch a dev server and watch for file changes, ng generate to add new files using Angular's built-in boilerplates, and finally, ng lint and ng test to run Angular's linting and unit testing tools.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=77)** There are three more tools you should know about in addition to the command line tool.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=81)** The Angular Language Service tells code editors how to work with Angular code.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=86)** Many popular text editors, like Visual Studio Code, already include support for Angular projects, but you can always add support by installing the correct extension or plugin for your IDE of choice, if available.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=98)** Every text editor is a little different, so check the documentation for your text editor for details on Language Service support.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=105)** Angular Dev Tools is a browser extension that gives you detailed debugging info in real time when running your application in a browser.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=113)** And finally, there's Schematics, which are collections of custom codes you want to reuse across multiple projects.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=119)** For example, the ng generate command uses Schematics under the hood to populate new files with the correct boilerplate content.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=128)** The Angular team does everything they can to streamline the process of writing code, deploying apps, and managing multiple projects.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/workflow-and-tooling?u=76281980&t=135)** I recommend you check out the official documentation for all the ways you can use Angular's ecosystem in your work.

### 2. Core Building Blocks

#### Angular decorators
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Decorators are functions that return functions.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=3)** They're not specific to the Angular framework, but they are a core piece of all Angular projects.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=8)** You cannot use Angular without using decorators.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=11)** Decorators in Angular supply metadata about a piece of code telling Angular what the code should do, and how it should inject that code into other parts of the application.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=20)** Decorators are marked by placing an @ symbol in front of the decorator name.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=24)** They're invoked at runtime, and expect arguments to be passed in either between the parentheses or immediately after the closing parentheses of the decorator.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=32)** This is why semicolons are left off the end of the decorator expressions and why parentheses even empty ones, always follow the decorator's name.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=41)** Common built-in decorators are NgModule to define modules.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=45)** Component to define components.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=47)** Injectable to define services.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=50)** Pipe to define pipes.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=53)** Directive to define directives, and input and output to define properties that send and receive data from the dom.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=61)** We'll look at some of these decorators in detail later in this course.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=65)** There are many built-in decorators available in Angular, and many properties on each decorator.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=69)** I recommend reading Angular official documentation for more info on all the decorators, and how they work in Angular projects.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=77)** Common decorator errors include missing parentheses, missing properties, missing values, and adding a semicolon at the end of the decorator statement.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=87)** In older versions of Angular, decorator errors were hard to diagnose because Angular emitted errors to the console on execution and not a compile time.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=96)** It's much easier to find issues in newer versions of Angular because of helpful feedback from the compiler.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=102)** Use the ng build and ng serve commands that come with the Angular CLI tool to take advantage of the error reporting.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=110)** Ng build compiles your code, and halts if there are errors.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=114)** Ng serve compiles your code, reports errors, and watches for changes.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-decorators?u=76281980&t=119)** Code editors like Visual Studio Code are also helpful because they can reason about your files, and give you real time feedback without compiling the code.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** find (1)
> **Env Vars:** cli (1)
> **Cross-References:** later in (1)
> **Tools:** visual studio (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Angular modules
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Angular modules represent a collection of closely related files that work together.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=5)** This might include things like components, services, or even pipes.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=9)** They help Angular figure out how all the parts of your application work together.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=14)** Starting in Angular version 14, you can use something called standalone components, which lets you use files in your project without assigning them to a module.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=23)** We'll talk more about standalone components later in this course.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=26)** As of this recording, standalone components are still new, and most existing projects will likely use modules to organize their code.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=33)** This is why all Angular developers should know how to use modules.

>

>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=73)** Angular modules are always TypeScript classes.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=76)** We use the NGModule decorator, here on line nine, to declare a class as a module.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=82)** I talked about decorators earlier in this course.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=85)** For now, just think of them as metadata that tells Angular what your code should do and how to inject that code into the rest of the application.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=92)** The NGModule decorator takes a configuration object as its only argument.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=98)** The declaration's property is an array of all the components, directives, and pipes that belong to this module.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=104)** In our case, we have only one file, the app component.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=108)** You can declare as many items as you need here, but keep in mind that code belongs to only one module at a time.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=114)** You'll get a compiler error if you try to assign code to more than one module.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=120)** The imports property is an array of all the external modules we want to make available inside this module's template.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=126)** Here, we import the AppRoutingModule, which gives us access to Angular's page navigation features.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=133)** The BrowserModule, which is all the low-level infrastructure Angular needs in all projects.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=139)** The common module, which has all of Angular's built-in pipes and directives, and the HTTP client module, which gives us access to the HTTP client service for making fetch requests.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=151)** The next config option is called exports, and it's an array of all the code in this module we want to make available for use in other modules.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=159)** This module doesn't export anything, which is why the value is an empty array.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=165)** The provider's property is an array of all the code we want to make available in this module through Angular's dependency injection.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=171)** This is different from importing a module for use in the template.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=175)** As a general rule, you put modules in the imports array and things like services in the providers array.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=181)** We'll talk more about services later in this course.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=185)** Finally, here's a special property called bootstrap.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=188)** This property tells Angular which components to load when you launch your application.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=192)** In our case, we load only one component, the app component.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=197)** When using a module architecture, you must have at least one module in your project.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=202)** We call this module the root module, and this is the only place you should ever declare a bootstrap property.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=209)** All properties in the module config are optional, which means you can leave properties with empty arrays, like this, or get rid of them, like this.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=217)** Both styles are correct and you can use whichever style you like.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=221)** I'll reset it to keep the empty arrays.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=225)** We export the module class itself on line 23.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=228)** Our module class is called AppModule, and it's an empty class.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=232)** You could use this class to do work very early in the bootstrap process, but that's rare.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=237)** I've never seen it.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=238)** This class is usually empty because all it needs to do is tell Angular which components to load on app launch.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=244)** To actually launch the application, we export the module class here and import it into the main.ts file here.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=252)** We pass the AppModule into this bootstrapModule method on Line six.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=258)** Bootstrap module does all the work of actually loading our code into memory and displaying components in the dom.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=264)** The bootstrap module function can take a configuration object as an optional second argument.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=269)** This is where you might set global settings like view encapsulation, or a missing translation strategy.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-modules?u=76281980&t=276)** Check out Angular's official documentation to find out more about all the things you can do with Angular modules and the bootstrap process.
>

#### Angular component decorator
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Speaker] Angular components are the main building blocks in all Angular applications.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=4)** You can't use Angular without using components and all Angular developers should know what they are and how to use them.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=11)** Components have four parts; an HTML template that defines the UI for the component, CSS styles you want to apply to the template.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=19)** A TypeScript class that controls the component's behavior and a component selector that tells Angular where to put the component in the DOM.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=28)** In my work, we use components to do mostly two things; to build the pages that users navigate to and to build small bits of UI we might want to reuse across multiple pages in the app.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=40)** We write components in the same way no matter how we use them and what we talk about in this video will work for any use case you might have.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=47)** Let's take a look at a component controller.

> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=80)** Always use the component.ts ending when naming your components controller file.

>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=105)** I talked about decorators earlier in this course.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=108)** For now, just think of them as metadata that tells Angular what your coach should do and how to inject it into the rest of your application.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=116)** The component decorator takes a configuration object as its only argument.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=120)** The selector property tells Angular where to use the component in the DOM.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=125)** While you could use any valid CSS selector as the target, I usually see a custom HTML element like this one here, app dash players.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=135)** Make sure you have at least one dash in your custom element name.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=138)** This is a convention that helps prevent naming collisions with standard HTML elements like button or input.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=146)** The template URL property tells Angular which file to use as the HTML for this component.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=153)** You can use any relative or absolute path here but templates are usually saved in the same folder as the rest of the components code.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=160)** You might see a template property here instead of a template URL property.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=164)** The only difference is template URL points to another file.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=168)** While template is the HTML itself.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=170)** Here's what that looks like.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=173)** I'll delete the template URL property, add a template property and set its value to some HTML, maybe a header.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=183)** You can use template or template URL but not both at the same time.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=188)** I recommend using the template URL property when possible to keep things consistent.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=193)** I'll just reset our component here to use that property.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=198)** The style URLs property is an array of files that define CSS styles for this component.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=205)** Again, you can use any relative or absolute URL here but the CSS files usually are saved in the same folder as the rest of the components code.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=215)** You might also see a Styles property.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=217)** The only difference is that Style URLs points to another file while Styles is an array of CSS itself.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=225)** That might look something like this.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=227)** I'll add the Styles property here under the Style URLs property and use a string to define some CSS.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=234)** Maybe set a color.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=239)** Unlike the template properties, you can use the styles and style URLs property together at the same time.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=246)** You can also use global CSS as long as the component doesn't use a shadow DOM.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=251)** As of this recording, Angular components don't use Shadow DOM by default so you should be able to use global CSS in most use cases.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=259)** I'll reset our component here to use just the style URLs property.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-decorator?u=76281980&t=264)** There are many more options available on the component decorator and I recommend you check out Angular's official documentation for details on all the things you can do.

>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=61)** There's a lot you can do with Angular's template syntax.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=64)** Our template here uses only a few of the most common things you'll find when working in an Angular project.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=70)** As a quick side note you can use any CSS you want inside an Angular project.

>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=80)** Bootstrap is a popular open source toolkit and I've used it for years but Angular will work just fine without Bootstrap and you can use any CSS that works well for your needs.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=90)** I'm going to scroll to the right here and on line two, we have something called a template reference variable, which is this hash symbol, also called a pound sign, followed by the word filter.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=103)** Template reference variables or template variables for short are a quick way to use data from one part of a template in other parts of the same template without having to pass that data into the components controller class.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=116)** Template variables always start with a hash and are followed by a custom string.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=121)** They always reference the element they are attached to and give us access to that element's DOM structure and data.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=128)** In our case here, we use the template variable to access the value of the input when users type a search string into the UI.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=136)** We then pass this value into the keyup binding here on the same line.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=141)** There are three common types of data binding in Angular, event binding, property binding, and interpolation.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=148)** Event binding lets us quickly assign actions to DOM events like keyup and click.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=154)** We declare event bindings with parentheses like these here.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=159)** We put the name of the event inside the parentheses, keyup in our case, and assign one or more actions to run when that event happens on this element.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=168)** Here, we pass the value of the template reference variable into the update method on our components controller class.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=177)** The method filters the list of players and returns a new list back to the DOM.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=182)** On line three, we bind to the click event here.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=187)** This binding does two things.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=188)** First, it passes an empty string into the update method which returns all the player names, and second it sets the value of the template reference variable to an empty string.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=200)** This resets the user input.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=203)** Property binding lets us dynamically assign values to the attributes on a standard HTML element or to properties on an Angular component.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=212)** We declare property bindings with square brackets like the ones here on line seven.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=219)** We put the name of the attribute or property inside the square brackets and assign it a value.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=224)** In our case, we use a property binding for our custom app online status directive and assign it the player.online property from our list of players.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=234)** Angular will set the value of this attribute dynamically at runtime for each player in our list.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=241)** We also use a property binding on line eight to set a dynamic URL for the anchor tag.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=248)** We'll talk more about app routing later in this course.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=253)** Interpolation lets us display dynamic content as text in the UI.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=258)** We declare interpolated content by using double curly braces and we put a reference to the value we want to display inside those braces.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=268)** Here we use interpolation to get the value of the player.name property for each player in our list and display it as text for that list item in the DOM.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=279)** Each player has a unique name and Angular will set the value of the list item dynamically at runtime for each player in the list.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=288)** There are several HTML templates in our example app and I recommend you take some time and explore the code to see how we use data binding and reference variables to make content interactive and dynamic.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/reference-variables-and-data-binding-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=298)** You can also check out the exercise files for links to more info.

#### Structural directives in Angular templates
