> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=42)** Always use the component.html ending for the file names for your component templates.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=48)** The Angular CLI tool does this for you when you generate new components using the NG generate component command.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=54)** You can use any valid HTML for a component's template.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=58)** Angular extends this HTML with its own custom attributes and syntax to make the content interactive and dynamic.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=65)** There's a lot you can do with Angular's template syntax.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=67)** Our template here uses only a few of the most common things you'll find when working in an Angular project.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=74)** As a quick side note, you can use any CSS you want inside an Angular project.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=78)** The CSS class names used in this template here all come from Bootstrap CSS.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=83)** Bootstrap is a popular open source toolkit and I've used it for years.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=87)** But Angular will work just fine without Bootstrap and you can use any CSS that works well for your needs.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=93)** Structural directives.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=95)** Change the layout of your UI by adding and removing DOM elements.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=99)** These directives always start with an asterisk followed by the directive name.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=104)** We use two common structural directives here on line six and seven, ngIf and ngFor.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=112)** Use the ngIf directive to add or remove DOM elements based on a Boolean value.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=118)** NgIf adds the DOM element if the condition is true and it removes the element if the condition is false.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=125)** You can use an optional else binding to specify an alternate template to use when the ngIf conditional is falsey.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=133)** Here in our template, we use the else binding to reference an alternate template called loading.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=138)** We define the loading template on lines 12 through 14 using an NG template element and the hash loading template reference variable.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=147)** NG template is a special component that comes with the Angular framework.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=151)** It lets you define a template that doesn't render by default.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=156)** The template needs a trigger of some kind to render the content.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=159)** In our case here, that trigger is the else binding inside the ngIf directive on line six.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=165)** If the ngIf directive is falsey, Angular will render the content of this loading component.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=172)** Here, it's just a paragraph with a loading message, but the alternate template could be any valid HTML code.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=179)** Use the ngFor directive to iterate over items in an array.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=183)** We use this directive on line seven to loop over the player's array on our components controller.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=189)** Use the let and of keywords to assign a variable name to items in the array.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=194)** You can then use this variable name to access the data inside each array item.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=199)** So in our case, we tell the ngFor directive to use the keyword player for each item in the array called players.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=210)** For each item in this array, we assign the value of player.online to our custom app online status directive and display the value of player.name as the content of each list item.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=224)** Let's see what all this looks like in the browser.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=227)** Inside our code space, I'll open a new terminal window and use the NPM start command to launch a dev server.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=235)** This might take some time to run, so please be patient.

> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=330)** This is an important point to remember.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=332)** NgIf doesn't just change the visibility of DOM elements.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=335)** It actually adds or removes them from the DOM.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=339)** I'll reset the values for our player's property so we can see the list of players again.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/structural-directives-in-angular-templates?u=76281980&t=346)** NgIf and ngFor are powerful structural directives that give you a lot of control of the content in your UI.
>

#### Angular standalone components
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As of Angular version 14 you can use something called standalone components which lets you use files in your project without assigning them to a module.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=10)** As of this recording, standalone components are still new and most existing projects will likely use modules to organize their code but standalone components are a powerful tool that make your code simpler and easier to work with.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=22)** All angular developers should know how to use standalone components.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=26)** I opened our project here in a GitHub code space.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=29)** I talked about why we use code spaces earlier in this course, so for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=39)** Let's take a look at some code.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=40)** I'll go to the source folder.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=42)** Open the app folder, components, high scores, and click on high scores.component.TS to open the file.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=51)** To mark any component, pipe, or directive as standalone, Add the standalone property to the decorator for that file like we do here on line 14.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=63)** As of Angular version 16, this property is false by default so you must set the property as true in each file where you want to use standalone components.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=73)** You can also use the dash dash standalone flag when generating new files using angulars command line tool.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=81)** Standalone components must declare their own imports and providers.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=85)** The imports property is an array of all the directives, components, pipes and modules you want to use in this components template.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=95)** Here on line 15, we import the common module which has all of angulars built-in pipes and directives.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=101)** The online status directive which is a custom directive that adds HTML to the DOM when a given player is online, right now.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=111)** The router link with H ref module which comes from the Angular router package and lets us define anchor links to navigate between pages in our app and the sort by score module which is a custom pipe for sorting a list of players by high score.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=128)** The provider's property, is an array of all the code we want to make available in this component through angulars dependency injection.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=136)** I talk about dependency injection in detail later in this course.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=140)** For now, just know that it's a software design pattern and a core concept in the Angular framework.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=146)** Our component doesn't have any injected dependencies so we can get rid of this property or leave it as an empty array like you see here.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=154)** To actually use this standalone component, we export the class here on line 19 and import it into other files as needed.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=162)** In our case, we use this component in the leaderboards page, which is an angular module.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=168)** I'll go to the source app, pages, leaderboards folder and click on leaderboards.module.TS to open the file.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=179)** Standalone components are backwards compatible with angular modules.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=182)** This means you can start using standalone components without changing your entire code base.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=187)** You can see the high scores component on line 18 in the imports array, for this module.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=192)** We would do the same thing if we used it in other components, directives or pipes.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=197)** Testing standalone components is very similar to testing module based components.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=201)** Let me show you what that looks like.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=203)** I'll go back to source, app, components, high scores, and click on highscores.component.spec.TS to open the file.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=213)** We look at unit testing in detail later in this course.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=216)** For now, just know that testing standalone components are mostly the same as testing any other file.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=222)** The big difference is how you inject the dependencies.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=225)** Angular comes with a collection of functions that help you set up dependencies without using modules.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=230)** We use two of these functions here on lines 15 and 16.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=235)** The provide HTTP client function configures angulars HTTP client service for our project and the provide HTTP client testing function mocks API calls when we run our unit tests.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=249)** You would use a similar pattern for other dependencies like angulars router, or service workers.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=255)** You might also use these types of functions when bootstrapping your real application or injecting dependencies into other parts of your project.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=263)** Take a moment and think about where you might be able to use standalone components in your project.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=267)** Any place where you don't absolutely need an angular module might be a good candidate for a standalone component.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-standalone-components?u=76281980&t=274)** Remember to check out the exercise files for links to more info.

#### Angular without ZoneJS (Zoneless)
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-without-zonejs-zoneless?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-without-zonejs-zoneless?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Starting in Angular version 18, you can opt in for a new zoneless architecture.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-without-zonejs-zoneless?u=76281980&t=6)** This is a huge change because Angular's been using ZoneJS since version 2.0.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-without-zonejs-zoneless?u=76281980&t=12)** The introduction of zoneless Angular means fewer change detection cycles, smaller app bundle sizes, faster startup times, better debugging, and better compatibility with third-party APIs.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-without-zonejs-zoneless?u=76281980&t=24)** The biggest risk is that as of this recording, zoneless Angular is an experimental feature.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-without-zonejs-zoneless?u=76281980&t=30)** The API is incomplete, unstable, and could change at any time.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-without-zonejs-zoneless?u=76281980&t=34)** It also requires changes to your project dependencies and may require refactoring of code and unit tests to make them compatible with the new API.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-without-zonejs-zoneless?u=76281980&t=42)** Check out Angular's official documentation for more info.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Versions:** version 18 (1), version 2 (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), is an  (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Code Keywords:** require (1)
> **Speakers:** - [narrator] (1)


### 3. Common Patterns

#### Overview of Angular pipes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Pipes are one of Angular's most powerful features.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=3)** They're easy to write, they're easy to test, and they keep your application fast and performant.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=8)** All Angular developers should know how to use pipes.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=12)** You can use pipes anytime you need to manipulate content in the UI.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=15)** This could be something simple, like making text uppercase or localizing numbers and date formats.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=20)** but you can also use pipes for more complex things, like joining an array of data into one big string or parsing error codes into a more meaningful description.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=30)** I love pipes because they meet all my criteria for great code.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=33)** They do one thing well.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=35)** I can compose them together, passing the output of one pipe as the input to another, and they're pure functions by default.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=42)** Let's talk about that last point real quick.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=44)** Pure functions, by definition, always return the same output for the same set of inputs.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=49)** This means if you already ran the code for a set of inputs, you don't need to run the code again because you already know the result.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=57)** Angular takes full advantage of this to make change detection faster and more performant.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=62)** We'll talk about change detection later in this course.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=64)** For now, just know that by default, Angular executes pipes only if there was a change in a primitive value, like a string or a Boolean, or if there was a change in a reference to an object or an array.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=76)** Angular ignores changes to data inside objects and arrays by default because it's much faster to check primitives and references than it is to do a deep scan on every change detection cycle.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=88)** With pure pipes, you must replace the reference to the object or array to run the pipe again.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=94)** You can also mark a pipe as impure, and Angular will execute that pipe every time it detects a keyboard or mouse input.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=100)** But impure pipes can slow down your app, so use them with caution.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=104)** I recommend sticking with pure pipes whenever possible.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=107)** Take a moment and think about where you might be able to use pipes in your project.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=111)** Any time you manipulate data before showing it in the UI might be a good candidate for a pipe.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=117)** As of this recording, Angular includes 13 standard pipes with all new projects.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=122)** These pipes help format things, like dates, numbers, and text.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=125)** We'll look at some of these pipes later in this course.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=128)** Check out the Exercise Files for links to more info.

#### Using custom Angular pipes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Earlier in this course, we looked at Angular pipes and why you might use them.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=4)** In this video, we'll look at one of our three custom pipes and how we built it.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=9)** I opened our project here in a GitHub code space.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=12)** I talked about why we use code spaces earlier in this course, so for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=22)** Let's take a look at our custom pipe.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=24)** I'll go to the source folder, open up the app folder, pipes, sort-by-score, and click on sort-by-score.pipe.ts to open the file.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=35)** Always use the pipe.ts ending for your pipe files.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=40)** The Angular CLI tool does this for you when you generate new pipes using the ng generate pipe command.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=47)** Angular pipes are always TypeScript classes.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=50)** We use the @Pipe decorator here on line 4 to declare a class has an Angular pipe.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=55)** We looked at decorators earlier in this course in detail.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=58)** For now, just think of them as metadata that tells Angular what your code should do and how to inject it into the rest of the application.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=66)** The @Pipe decorator takes a configuration object as its only argument.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=70)** The name property tells Angular how you will reference the pipe in your templates.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=75)** It's good practice to use camel case when naming your pipes.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=78)** The Angular CLI tool does this for you when you generate new pipe files using the CLI command.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=84)** Avoid hyphens in the name property.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=87)** Angular reserves that character for components and directives.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=90)** You'll get a build error if you try to use hyphens for a pipe name.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=95)** The pure property tells Angular if the pipe is pure or not.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=99)** I talk about the difference between pure and non-pure pipes earlier in this course.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=103)** So for now, just know that Angular pipes are pure by default to make change detection faster and more performant.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=110)** This property is optional, and you can leave it out of the decorator to use Angular's default settings.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=116)** The last option is the standalone property.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=119)** If your project uses Angular modules, you can leave this property out of the decorator to use Angular's default settings.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=125)** As of this recording, you must set the standalone property as true here in the pipe if your project uses a standalone component architecture.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=134)** I talked about standalone components in detail earlier in this course.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=137)** For now, just know that modules are still the default behavior when generating new custom pipes.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=143)** We export the pipe class itself on line 9.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=147)** You can call your pipe class as anything you like, but it's good practice to end the class name with the word pipe.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=153)** And while not technically required, it's also good practice to implement the PipeTransform interface from the Angular core package.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=160)** This helps with autocompletion and type safety.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=164)** The most important part of any pipe is the transform method.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=169)** Angular looks for a method with this name and errors out if it can't find it.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=174)** The transform method takes at least one argument.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=177)** The first argument is always the value from the template you want to change.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=181)** In our case, this will be the list of players we want to sort.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=186)** Additional arguments are always the extra parameters you want to pass into the pipe, if any.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=191)** You must always return a value from a pipe, even if the value is null or undefined.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=197)** You'll get a build error if there's no return value.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=200)** If your project uses a module architecture, you must declare your custom pipes in at least one module to use them.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=207)** If your project uses standalone components, you don't need to declare the pipe in a module file.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=212)** This pipe has its own module called sort-by-score.module.ts.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=217)** I'll open that now.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=220)** We add the SortByScorePipe class to the declarations array on line 7 and export the same class on line 13.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=228)** This makes it available for use in other modules.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=231)** You can then import this SortByScoreModule class into other modules as needed.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=237)** I love using pipes, and I recommend you and your team experiment with them as much as you can.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=242)** Pipes can do any kind of transformation you need, but it's good practice to write pipes that do only one thing.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=249)** This makes them easy to test.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=251)** It also makes them easy to chain together in the template.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=254)** The output of one pipe becomes the input of the next.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=257)** Pipes that do too much are hard to chain together.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=260)** But doing one thing well doesn't mean you're limited to only simple things like sorting arrays.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=267)** Take a moment and think about your own project.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=269)** Any place where you manipulate data before showing it in the UI might be a good candidate for a pipe.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-custom-angular-pipes?u=76281980&t=276)** Check out the exercise files for links to more info.

#### Angular services and the injectable decorator
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=0)** - Angular services are a way to separate and reuse code across different parts of your application.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=6)** You don't need to use services to use Angular but I've seen them in every project I've worked on and all Angular developers should be familiar with how they work.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=15)** Services work best for things that don't change the UI directly.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=19)** This might be things like API calls or complex calculations.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=23)** This keeps your components small and focused only on showing data in the UI.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=28)** Services are also flexible.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=30)** You can inject services into other services.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=33)** You can inject services into components.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=35)** You can inject services into the entire app or only into one or two specific places.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=40)** The choice is yours.

> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=105)** We use the Injectable Decorator here on line six to declare a class as an Angular service.
>

>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=121)** The Injectable Decorator takes a configuration object as its only argument the provided in property tells Angular what parts of your project have access to this service.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=132)** By default, the value of this property is null which means you must add the service to a module to use it.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=139)** In my work, we usually set the value as root like you see here which tells Angular to create one instance of the service for all parts of the app.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=148)** The CLI tool does this for you when you generate new services using the CLI command having one instance of a service is a powerful feature of how Angular's Dependency Injection works.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=158)** Since the project has only one instance of the service, sometimes called a singleton, all parts of the app have access to the same data stored in the service.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=167)** This makes it easy to cache values or shared data between pages.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-services-and-the-injectable-decorator?u=76281980&t=171)** Check out the exercise files for links to more info.

#### Angular service classes

>

>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=57)** You can call your service classes anything you'd like, but it's good practice to end the class name with the word service.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=64)** Lines 11 through 13 are the constructor for our service class.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=68)** This is where we inject dependencies and initialize code.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=72)** Here we use the constructor to inject Angular's HTTP client service as a private class member.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=79)** We don't have any code to initialize, so we leave the body of the constructor empty.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=84)** I like to use private class members whenever possible to make it easy to delete dead code, but you can use public or protected class members if that works better for you.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=93)** If you're using a module architecture, remember to import the HTTP client module into your main app module to get access to the HTTP client class.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=103)** We looked at modules and how to use them earlier in this course.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=108)** The get all players method on line 15 is how we communicate with the database to get the info about all the players.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=117)** Putting the dollar sign at the end of the method name is a standard convention when working with observables in Angular.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=123)** We'll talk more about observables and reactive programming later in this course.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=127)** For now, just think of them as a type of promise, but for data that returns more than once.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=132)** It's not required, but I like using the dollar sign because it makes it clear that the method is an observable and I'll need to unwrap it to get to the data.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=142)** On line 16, we use the instance of Angular's HTTP client to make a get request to the database for our data.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=150)** The HTTP instance gives us access to all the rest methods we need to work with the database.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=156)** The get method takes two arguments.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=158)** The first argument is a string representing the URL for our data.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=162)** This can be any valid URL including a local URL like we have here.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=168)** The second argument is an object of optional request settings.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=172)** We don't have any custom settings for this request, so we don't need it here.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=176)** While not required, it's also a good idea to set the return type of the get method like this.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=183)** This helps with auto completion and type safety.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=186)** We'll get a generic object back from the get method if we don't set the return value.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=192)** This pipe method here, chained to our get request, is a built-in for working with observables.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=197)** It lets you transform data inside the observable by composing functions together.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=203)** The output of each function becomes the input of the next.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=205)** Here we pipe the response from our get request into a function called delay, which we imported from the RXJS library.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=213)** We're using delay here to simulate network lags so we can see the loading states in our app.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=218)** In my work, we usually use the pipe method to map and filter data as well as catch errors.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=225)** The next two methods on our service, get players by ID and get players by name, do just that.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=232)** On line 20, we call the get all players method and pipe it into the RXJS map function on line 21.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=241)** This takes a callback function that gives us access to the data inside the observable.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=247)** We then iterate over each item in the list of players and return the first player that matches the ID.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=254)** We do something similar on line 27.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=257)** We use the RXJS map function to get access to the data inside the observable and then iterate over each item in the list of players filtering the list to include only the players that match by name.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=271)** Keep in mind that this is just one way to write an Angular service.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=274)** You can use services for all sorts of complex calculations or business logic.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=280)** To actually use the service, we use Angular's dependency injection to inject it into the classes that need them.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=287)** We'll talk more about dependency injection later in this course.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=290)** For now, just know that it's a software design pattern where classes or functions request dependencies from an external source rather than creating their own.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=299)** Let me show you what that looks like.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=300)** I'll go to source, app, pages, players, and click on players dot component dot TS to open the file.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=311)** This is the controller for our players page.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=314)** We import the API service on line four and inject it into the class constructor on line 15 as a private member.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=322)** We then use the service on lines 19 and 23 to update the list of players as needed.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=329)** We use this pattern several times throughout the example app.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=332)** I recommend you take some time and explore the code to see how all the pages and components interact with the API service.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-service-classes?u=76281980&t=339)** And remember to check out the exercise files for links to more info.

#### Angular directive decorator
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Directives let you attach custom behavior to elements in your Angular templates.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=5)** All Angular developers should be familiar with directives and how they work.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=9)** This behavior can be as simple or as complex as you need it to be.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=13)** I remember one project where we wrote a custom directive for an entire secret menu full of tools used by the dev team.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=20)** Using a directive meant we could access this secret menu from anywhere in the app.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=25)** I opened our project here in a GitHub Codespaces.

>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=30)** So for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=37)** Let's take a look at our custom directive.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=39)** I'll go to the src folder, open the app folder, directives, click on online-status.directive.ts to open the file.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=49)** Always use the directive.ts ending for your directive file names.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=53)** The Angular CLI tool does this for you when you generate new directives using the ng generate directive command.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=60)** Angular directives are always TypeScript classes.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=63)** We use the directive decorator here on line three to declare a class as a directive.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=69)** I talk about decorators earlier in this course, so for now just think of them as metadata that tells Angular what your code should do and how to inject it into the rest of your application.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=79)** The directive decorator takes a configuration object as its only argument.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=85)** The selector property tells Angular what CSS selector to use to identify the directive in your templates.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=92)** You can use any valid CSS selector, but I usually see custom directives use an attribute selector like we do here.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=101)** The provider's property is an array of all the code we want to make available in this directive through Angular's dependency injection.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=108)** I talk about dependency injection in detail later in this course.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=112)** For now, just know that it's a software design pattern and a core concept in the Angular framework.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=117)** Our directive here doesn't have any injected dependencies so we can get rid of the property if we want to or leave it as an empty array like you see here.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=126)** Another option is the standalone property.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=129)** If your project uses Angular modules, you can leave this property out of the decorator to use Angular's default settings.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=136)** As of this recording, you must set the standalone property as true here in the directive if your project uses a standalone component architecture.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=145)** I talked about standalone components in detail earlier in this course.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=148)** For now, just know that modules are still the default behavior when generating new custom directives.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=154)** What you see here are the properties I use most often in my work.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-directive-decorator?u=76281980&t=158)** There are lots more ways to configure directives and I recommend you check out the exercise files for links to more info.

### 4. Coding Essentials

#### Angular project configuration
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In Angular, the two main configuration files are tsconfig.json and angular.json.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=7)** All Angular developers should be familiar with these files.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=11)** I opened our project here in a GitHub Codespace.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=14)** I talked about why we use Codespaces earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=16)** So for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine, I'll go to the root of our project and click on tsconfig.json to open the file.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=29)** We write Angular apps using TypeScript.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=31)** And this file tells the TypeScript compiler how to generate JavaScript code.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=36)** The Angular CLI tool configures sensible defaults when you start a new project, but you can always change the settings if needed.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=43)** The two properties I customize most often are compiler options and Angular compiler options.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=52)** Use the compiler options property to tell TypeScript how to compile your code.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=58)** And use the Angular compiler options property to define TypeScript options specific to compiling Angular projects.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=67)** You may have more than one tsconfig file, like we do here.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=72)** That's okay.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=73)** This lets you have more than one configuration depending on what you need.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=77)** Here, we have this main tsconfig file, as well as separate configurations for building the app and running our suite of unit tests.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=87)** There are lots of ways to customize your tsconfig files, and I recommend you check out the official documentation for more info.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=94)** Next, let's take a look at angular.json, which is also in the root of the project.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=101)** This file is where you tell Angular CLI how to configure and build your project.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=105)** Again, this file is full of sensible defaults when you start a new project using the CLI tool.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=112)** Use the projects property to define all the apps in your workspace, as well as build targets.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=118)** A workspace is just a collection of one or more projects you want to manage through this one configuration file.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=124)** In my work, we usually have one Angular app per workspace, but your project may be different and you may choose to break your code into many smaller implementations.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=134)** Build targets lets you change the configuration and optimization of your code depending on where you want to deploy it.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=141)** Angular comes with production and development targets by default, but you can add or remove targets as needed.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angularpproject-configuration?u=76281980&t=153)** Again, there are lots of ways to customize Angular's workspace settings, so be sure to check out the exercise files for links to more info.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### Injecting dependencies using dependency injection
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Dependency Injection, DI for short, is a software design pattern where classes and functions request dependencies from an external source rather than creating their own instances.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=12)** It's not specific to the Angular framework, but it's a core concept and every Angular developer should know how it works.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=18)** You can't use Angular without DI.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=21)** I opened our project here in a GitHub code space.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=24)** I talk about why we use code spaces earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=27)** So for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=33)** I'll go to the source folder, open the app folder, pages, profile, and click on profile.component.ts to open the file.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=44)** This is the component controller for our profile page.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=47)** We looked at components in detail earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=50)** For now, just know that they're the main building blocks for all Angular applications.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=55)** To use the dependency, we declare it in the class constructor as an argument.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=60)** There's a few ways to do this depending on what you inject.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=63)** For services, you can use the shorthand syntax you see here on lines 21 and 22.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=69)** The private keyword comes from TypeScript.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=72)** It tells Angular that these dependencies are for internal use only and should not be made available outside the class.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=78)** As of this recording, the keyword is optional.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=81)** You can leave it off like this.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=83)** And dependencies will be public by default.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=87)** Keep in mind that only public and protected class members are available in a components HTML template.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=93)** I like to use the private class members when possible to make it easy to delete dead code, but you could make these dependencies public or protected and access them directly in the template if you wanted to.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=103)** Take some time and experiment with the code to see which option works for you.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=108)** Next, after the private keyword is a unique name we use to reference the instance of each dependency.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=114)** You can use any name you like, but I like to keep things short and easy to understand.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=118)** On Line 21, I use api for the API service.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=122)** And on line 22, I use route for Angular's activated route service.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=128)** The next part is where the magic happens.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=131)** Right after each name, I add a type annotation.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=135)** Angular uses this type info to figure out which dependency we want and it automatically injects that dependency into our class.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=143)** Let's break this down.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=145)** Angular keeps a collection of key value pairs that represent all the dependencies in your app.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=151)** Angular calls this list an injector.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=154)** Angular stores a reference to each dependency using a unique key called a token.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=159)** When you use a token, Angular looks in the injector for an instance of that dependency and returns the instance if found.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=167)** In our case here, Angular sees the type annotation, checks the injector for an instance of the API service, an activated route service, and returns the instance for those dependencies.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=179)** Depending on how you define and declare dependencies, Angular can create only one instance for all parts of the app.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=186)** This is called a singleton and it lets all parts of your app have access to the same data inside the dependency.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=192)** You don't need to use singletons, but I recommend using them when you can.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=197)** Take a moment and think about where you might be able to use singletons in your project.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=201)** Anytime you need to cache data or share data between pages, it might be a good candidate for a singleton.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/injecting-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=208)** Check out the exercise files for links to more info.
