#### Declaring dependencies using dependency injection
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Earlier in this course, we looked at how to inject dependencies into your application using Angular's dependency injection.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=7)** In this video, we'll look at how to declare those dependencies to make them available for use.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=12)** I opened our project here in a GitHub Codespace.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=15)** I talked about why we use Codespaces earlier in this course, so for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=25)** I'll go to the src folder.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=26)** Open up the app folder, services and click on api.service.ts to open the file.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=34)** This is the code for our custom API Service.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=38)** Dependency injection and Angular services are closely linked.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=42)** You can't use one without the other and most of the time services are all you need but you can inject other things too like strings, booleans, and object literals, as long as you give Angular a provider for that dependency.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=55)** We'll look at providers in detail later in this course.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=57)** For now, just think of them like recipes that tell Angular how to make and inject an instance of a dependency.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=63)** We use the injectable decorator here on line 6 to declare a class as an injectable dependency.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=71)** Decorators are functions that return functions.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=74)** They're not specific to the Angular framework but they are a core piece of all Angular projects.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=79)** The injectable decorator tells Angular to register this class with something called the injector making it available for future use.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=87)** Think of the injector as a collection of key value pairs that represent all the dependencies in your app.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=93)** Angular stores a reference to each dependency using a unique key called a token.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=98)** When you use a token, Angular looks in the injector for an instance of that dependency and returns the instance if found.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=105)** The injectable decorator takes a configuration object as its only argument.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=110)** The providedIn property tells Angular what parts of your project have access to the dependency.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=116)** By default, the value of this property is null which means you must add the dependency directly to each module that uses it.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=123)** You might see the decorator without a configuration object like this.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=128)** This is the same thing as setting the providedIn property to no.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=133)** In my work, we usually set the value as root like this.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=139)** This tells Angular to create one instance of the service for all parts of the app.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=144)** The CLI tool does this for you when you generate new services using the ng generate service command.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=150)** Having only one instance of something is sometimes called a singleton.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=154)** It's a powerful tool that lets all parts of your app have access to the same data inside the dependency.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=160)** You don't need to use singletons but I recommend using them when you can to keep things consistent in your code base.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=165)** Take a moment and think about where you might be able to use singletons in your project.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=169)** Anytime you need to cache data or shared data between pages might be a good candidate for singleton.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=175)** There's another value called platform.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=180)** This creates one instance of the dependency shared across all Angular applications in your project.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=187)** I never use the platform value in my work because there's usually only one Angular app in each project.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=193)** I recommend using the root value when you can and safe platform for when you know you need it.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/declaring-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=199)** Check out the exercise files for links to more info.

>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=45)** For example, let's imagine that our API service was not already injected into the route of our project.
>

>

>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=72)** The provide property tells Angular how you'll reference this dependency in your code.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=77)** Angular calls this reference a token.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=79)** Tokens must be unique for each dependency.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=81)** In our case here, the token for our API service is the class itself but you can also use a string or something called an injection token if needed.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=90)** What you use for a token depends on what you need to inject and what the rest of your code base looks like.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=96)** The use class property tells Angular what to inject when you ask for this dependency using the token.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=101)** Here, we're telling Angular to inject the API service class.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=106)** Because the token and the thing we want to inject are the same, we can use the shorthand syntax you see on line 22.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=112)** This tells Angular, whenever you see the API service token inject the API Service class.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=118)** Dependency injection and Angular services are closely linked.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=122)** You can't use one without the other and most of the time, services are all you need.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=126)** So you'll see this shorthand syntax a lot.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=129)** Keep in mind, the shorthand syntax only works with classes but you can inject other things too as long as you give Angular a token and a value for that dependency.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=139)** The useValue property lets you inject primitives like strings, booleans and object literals.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=152)** The useFactory property lets you define a callback function that returns a value.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=161)** And the useExisting property is kind of like an alias for an existing token.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=165)** It basically tells Angular, hey, whenever you see this one token, use the value for this other token instead.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=173)** Again, how you define the value for a token depends on what you need to inject and what the rest of the code base looks like.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=180)** Here are two things to keep in mind when working with providers.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=183)** First, if you're using standalone components, everything works the same as with modules.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=187)** The only difference is that you add your dependencies to the provider's array and the component decorator instead of the module decorator.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=195)** We talked about standalone components in detail earlier in this course.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=199)** Second, if you inject your services into the route of your project, you don't need to declare them in a provider's array.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=206)** You can add them directly to your class instructors as needed.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/providing-dependencies-using-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=209)** We talked about injecting services earlier in this course so for now, just know that our API service is injected into the route of our project so we don't need to add it to the list of providers here.

#### Angular change detection strategies
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Change detection is how Angular checks your application state for changes and updates the UI to keep everything in sync.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=7)** It's what makes Angular applications dynamic and interactive.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=10)** You can't use Angular without change detection, and every Angular developer should know how it works.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=17)** I opened our project here in a GitHub code space.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=19)** I talk about why we use code spaces earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=22)** So for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=28)** I'll go to the source folder.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=30)** Open the app folder, components, high-scores, and click on the high-scores.component.ts file to open it.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=39)** All components use Angular's default change detection unless you tell the framework to use a different strategy.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=46)** As of this recording, there are only two strategies, default and something called OnPush.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=52)** You set the strategy on any component by adding the change detection property in that component's decorator, like we do here on line 17.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=61)** Angular's default change detection checks a component for changes whenever it mutates data inside objects and arrays, whenever it redefines properties on the component's class, or whenever it triggers events like API calls or click handlers.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=77)** Angular will then start at the root component and compare the new state tree with the old state tree using a strict equality comparison.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=85)** It marks the things that changed with a Boolean, and Angular reruns only the code that needs the changes.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=92)** When you set a component to use OnPush like we did here, Angular skips checking that component and all its sub trees.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=100)** This speeds up the change detection cycle because there's less code to check.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=105)** With OnPush, you must redefine a property, trigger an event, or manually call change detection to have Angular check for changes in that component and its sub trees.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=115)** We can use OnPush here in this component because we never mutate the data.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=119)** Instead, we declare this public players& property on line 20 and set its value only once on line 27.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=130)** The Angular team optimized change detection to take full advantage of the JavaScript engines inside modern browsers.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=136)** But onPush is a great option if your project has a lot of components to check for updates.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=141)** Take a moment and think about where you might be able to use OnPush in your project.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=145)** Any place where you have a lot of components that need updating might be a good candidate for OnPush change detection.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-change-detection-strategies?u=76281980&t=152)** Also, check out my audio course on building large-scale Angular projects for more info on Angular's change detection and how it works.

#### Angular component lifecycle hooks
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Lifecycle hooks lets you run custom code when Angular initializes a component or directive, when the template loads in the dom, when changes happen, and when Angular destroys the component.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=12)** These hooks are a big part of building Angular apps, and all Angular developers should know how to use them.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=19)** I opened our project here in a GitHub code space.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=22)** I talked about why we use code spaces earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=24)** So, for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=31)** I'll go to the source folder, open the app folder, pages, players, and click on players.component.ts to open the file.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=41)** As of this recording, Angular exposes eight lifecycle hooks for each component or directive.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=46)** You access these hooks by adding them as public methods on your component's class.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=51)** Each hook has a special method name, and each taps into a different part of the component lifecycle.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=56)** The most common hook I use in my work is ngOnInit.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=60)** You can see that method here starting on line 18.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=63)** Angular will call this method once after the initial construction of the component or directive.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=68)** While you could do something similar with the class constructor, using ngOnInit is a much safer option, because Angular can guarantee access to the dependency injector, as well as the component's inputs.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=80)** I recommend using ngOnInit when you can instead of using the class instructor.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=85)** While, not required, it's good practice to implement the interface for the lifecycle hooks you want to use.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=91)** This helps with auto completion and type safety.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=94)** You can implement as many hooks as you need by adding them as implementation details on your class.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=100)** We do that here on line 11.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=103)** Here, we implement only one hook, but I could add more hooks by adding them to this list separated by commas.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=110)** For example, if I wanted to also use the ngOnDestroy hook, another common hook I use in my work, I would implement it by typing a comma, followed by the interface name, which is on Destroy.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=126)** I would then add a new public method with the correct name.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=129)** Here, it would be ngOnDestroy.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=132)** And I would put whatever code I need inside this method.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=136)** Angular calls the ngOnDestroy method once just before it tears down a component or directive.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=142)** It's a safe place to do cleanup work, like unsubscribing from observables, stopping timers, and unregistering callbacks.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=150)** There's nothing in this particular component that needs cleanup, so I'll reset the code and get rid of the implementation.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=157)** Take a moment, and think about places in your project where you could use Angular's lifecycle hooks.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=162)** Any place where you need to tap into the key events of a component's lifecycle may be a good place to use a hook.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-component-lifecycle-hooks?u=76281980&t=169)** Check out the exercise files for links to more info.

> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=43)** There are two parts to using Angular Router in your app.

> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=53)** We'll look at using routes in your templates later in this course.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=57)** In my work, we usually define all our routes in one big array like you see here starting on line five.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=64)** It's an array of objects.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=66)** Each object represents one route definition.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=71)** You can have as many definitions as you need, but keep in mind that the order of your routes matter because Angular will always use the first route it finds that satisfies the parsing criteria.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=81)** Always put the most specific routes at the start of your array and the least specific routes at the end.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=90)** There are a few ways to define routes depending on your needs.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=93)** What you see here is the approach I use most often in my work.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=97)** The path property is a string that tells Angular what to look for in the URL.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=102)** This value is always relative to the parent route, if any.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=106)** If there's no parent route, then this value is relative to the base HREF defined in the index dot HTML file.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=113)** Let me show you that.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=116)** We define the base HREF on line six in the index dot HTML file, which is just the root of our website in this case.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=123)** But you can set this value to whatever you need.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=126)** Let's go back to our route definitions.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=129)** The value of the path property can also include something called a dynamic route parameter like you see on line seven, as well as wild cards like you see on line 33.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=144)** We'll look at dynamic route parameters later in this course.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=148)** Wild cards tell Angular to accept any value it sees in that position in the URL.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=154)** In my work, we use it most often to define a catchall route like you see here at the bottom of the array.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=160)** This definition tells Angular to use this route when no other route matches the URL.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=166)** Here we tell Angular to redirect to the all players page using the redirect to property.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=173)** The value of this property is a string.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=176)** This forward slash tells Angular that the string represents an absolute route relative to the base HREF.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=184)** No forward slash means the redirect is relative to the parent route, if any.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=190)** Another special path you might see is an empty string like here on line 28.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=197)** This path represents the route URL for your project, usually the same thing as the base HREF.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=204)** This special path is usually paired with the path match property.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=210)** Path match tells Angular which strategy to use when checking URLs.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=215)** Angular checks URLs left to right and by default, it will use the first route definition it finds that is a partial match for the URL.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=224)** Most of the time this behavior works like we expect it to, but sometimes, like here, we want Angular to match the route only if it's an exact match.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=232)** We do that by setting the path match property to the value of full.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=237)** This tells Angular to match this route only if it is an exact match with nothing else in the URL.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=244)** If we didn't set the path match property, Angular would match this route every time because every route starts with the root URL.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=253)** We use the load children property on lines 13 and 19 to tell Angular which module to use when navigating to that path.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=261)** You can do the same thing for standalone components using the load component property like you see on lines 8 and 24.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=272)** We'll look at these properties in detail later in this course.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=277)** The title property lets you set the page name displayed in the browser tab or browser window.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=283)** It's also the name used when a user bookmarks the page.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=286)** You can set the value as a static string like we do here on lines 14 and 20, or you can set the value as a function like we do on line 9.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=295)** Using a function gives you access to Angular's dependency injection, as well as access to the details of the current URL.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=302)** This lets you create a dynamic page title based on some custom calculation you define in the function.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=311)** We'll also look at this in detail later in the course.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=314)** Finally, there's this data property like you see on lines 15 and 25.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=321)** It lets you assign additional custom key value pairs to any route if needed.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=326)** You can then use this data in other parts of your app by accessing the property through the route object or the activate route class.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=333)** We'll look at these properties in detail later in this course.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/defining-routes-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=337)** Angular Router is a powerful tool and I recommend you check out the exercise files for links to more info on all the ways you can customize page navigation in your project.

>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-routes-with-angular-s-router-module?u=76281980&t=83)** ForRoot tells Angular that this is the main instance for your route definitions.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-routes-with-angular-s-router-module?u=76281980&t=88)** Call this method only once in your project and pass in your route definitions as the first argument.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-routes-with-angular-s-router-module?u=76281980&t=95)** The second argument is an optional configuration object which we'll look at in detail later in this course.

> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-routes-with-angular-s-router-module?u=76281980&t=101)** Use the forChild method to define a subset of routes for a specific module.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-routes-with-angular-s-router-module?u=76281980&t=106)** For example, we load the leaderboards module on line 19.

> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-routes-with-angular-s-router-module?u=76281980&t=144)** This method also takes an array of route definitions as its first argument, just like forRoute.

> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/using-routes-with-angular-s-router-module?u=76281980&t=150)** While not required, using forChild is a great way to organize your routing because each module can manage its own routes.

#### Lazy loading Angular routes

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=6)** In this video, we'll look at lazy loading components and modules to make your app faster and more performant.

>

>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=31)** Angular Router is the official package for managing routes in Angular apps.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=35)** It's an optional package that you add to your project as needed using the Angular CLI tool.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=41)** There are two parts to using Angular Router in your app.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=43)** The first part is defining your routes and bootstrapping the router service.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=48)** The second part is using the routes in your templates.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=51)** We'll look at using routes in your templates later in this course.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=56)** When defining a route, the loadChildren property tells Angular which module to use when navigating to this route.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=63)** You can do something similar with the loadComponent property to load standalone components like you see on lines 8 and 24.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=71)** Either way, the value for these properties should be a callback that returns a dynamic import.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=78)** Dynamic imports are a special function-like expression available in all modern browsers that lets you load ES6 modules in an asynchronous way.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=88)** This lets Angular lazy load your routes.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=91)** With lazy loading, Angular only loads modules when needed.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=94)** This is Angular's default behavior when working with routes and it usually means a faster initial page load which everyone likes.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=102)** The downside is that there's more network lag when routing between pages because Angular must fetch the next module before rendering it.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=109)** The slower the network connection, the more lag when navigating between pages.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=114)** But if you pair lazy loading with a preload strategy, Angular loads only the modules needed for that first page and then immediately makes background requests for some or all of the other modules.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=127)** This means you get the best of both worlds, a fast initial page load and low lag when navigating between pages.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=134)** Let me show you what that looks like in the browser.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=136)** Inside our code space, I'll open a new terminal window and use the npm start command to launch a dev server.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=146)** This may take a moment, so please be patient.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=150)** This alert here in the lower right-hand corner tells me I have a successful connection to the dev server inside the code space.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=157)** I can click this blue button to launch a new browser tab or I can command click on this URL here to do the same thing.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=165)** Either way, our project opens in a new tab so we can see our changes in real time.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=173)** With our example app running in a dev server, I'll go to the network tab here in Chrome's developer tools, clear the content of the tab, and I'll click on this button up here in the browser to refresh the page.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=185)** We can see all the network traffic now in that request including this file here which represents the module code for the current page.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=195)** There's also this file here that represents the messages page, which we loaded in the background using a preload strategy.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=204)** We'll look at preloading strategies in detail later in this course.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=208)** What you don't see here is any code for the leaderboards page.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=211)** Angular will lazy load that module only when we need it.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=215)** If I clear the network tab and navigate to the leaderboards page, you can see the network request to get the code for that page.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=227)** And if I navigate to the messages page, there's no new network request for component code because we already made that call and Angular has it in memory.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=239)** Take a moment and think about places in your project where you could take advantage of lazy loading for components and modules.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=246)** Any code you don't need right away when your app loads might be a good candidate for lazy loading.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/lazy-loading-angular-routes?u=76281980&t=251)** Be sure to check out the exercise files for links to more info.

#### Preloading strategies for Angular Router
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=7)** In this video, we'll focus on how you can use preloading strategies to load content in the background for navigating between pages.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=14)** I opened our project here in a GitHub codespace.

>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=38)** It's an optional package that you add to your project as needed using the Angular CLI tool.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=44)** There are two parts to using Angular Router in your app.
>

>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=51)** The second part is using the routes in your templates.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=54)** We'll look at using routes in your templates later in this course.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=57)** By default, Angular uses lazy loading to load components and modules only when needed.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=63)** This usually means a faster initial load time for your app, which everyone likes.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=67)** The downside is that there's more network lag when routing between pages because Angular must first fetch the next module before rendering it.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=76)** The slower the network connection, the more lag your users see when navigating between pages.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=82)** Most of the time, lazy loading works well but you can override Angulars default behavior by setting a preloading strategy for Angular Router.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=90)** Let me show you what that looks like.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=93)** Down here in line 40, in the routing module, we add Angular's Router module to the list of imports in the NgModule decorator.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=102)** We do this so we can pass in the route definitions and bootstrap the service.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=108)** We can also pass in an optional configuration object as the second argument that lets us set several options including this preloading strategy property.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=117)** The value of this property is a class that has a public method on it that returns an observable.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=123)** The default value is no preloading which means all routes are lazy loaded when needed.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=129)** You can use the built-in preload all modules class to load all available modules on app launch.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=135)** This works well for small apps but keep in mind that the more modules you load, the longer your app will take to launch.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=142)** One final option is to use a custom service to pick and choose which modules you want to load in the background.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=148)** That's what we did here with this PreloadingService.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=153)** Let's take a look at it in detail.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=154)** I'll go to src, app, services and click on preloading.service.ts to open the file.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=163)** This is a standard Angular service using the injectable decorator.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=168)** We looked at services in detail earlier in this course so for this video we'll focus on just the things that make it unique for managing our preload strategy.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=176)** Our custom class is called preloading service and you can see on line 8 that it implements Angular's preloading strategy class for the interface.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=186)** You don't need to implement this class but it's good practice and helps with auto completion and type safety.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=192)** What's important is that your service has a public method called Preload because that's the method name Angular will look for when checking all the routes to see which ones need to get loaded in the background.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=204)** Angular will pass each route definition into this method as the first argument.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=212)** The second argument is a callback function that returns an observable.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=216)** This is how you tell Angular to preload the route.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=220)** There are a few ways to do this and the technique you use depends a lot on the needs of your project, but I kept things simple here and just use the basic conditional statement to choose the route to preload.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=233)** On line 11, I checked to see if the route has a data object and if it does, I check the value of the preload property on that object.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=248)** If preload is true, I call the callback function.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=252)** If not, I create a new observable with a value of null.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=257)** We access the preload property using square bracket notation because of the type definition for the data object that comes from Angular.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=266)** This data object comes from the routes we defined in our app-routing.module.ts file.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=278)** Keep in mind that this is only one way to make a custom preloading service.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=281)** You can use any technique that works well for your needs.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=285)** Take a moment and think about places in your project where you could take advantage of preloading components and modules.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=291)** Any code you need right away when your app loads might be a good candidate for preloading.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/preloading-strategies-for-angular-router?u=76281980&t=296)** Check out the exercise files for links to more info.

### 6. Reactive Programming

#### Angular signals and the Signal function
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Starting in version 16, the Angular team introduced something called Signals that you can use to make your code more reactive.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=8)** Reactive programming is a coding pattern where you can watch for changes in data over time and trigger things to happen based on those changes.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=16)** This coding pattern is a big part of the Angular framework and all Angular developers should be familiar with Signals and how to use them.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=23)** Signals wrap your data inside getters and setters that Angular can easily track.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=28)** When a value changes, Angular can propagate that one change to any place in the code that needs it.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=34)** This is more efficient than Angular's other change detection strategies.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=38)** We looked at change detection in detail earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=41)** For now, just know that by default, Angular needs to check all the components and child components that might have changed before actually making that change.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=50)** With Signals, Angular updates just that one value and doesn't need to worry about other parts of the component.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=56)** As of this, recording, Signals are still in development preview, which means the API may change at any time based on feedback from the community.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=64)** Check out Angular's official documentation for the most up-to-date info on Signals and how to use them.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=70)** I opened our project here in a GitHub codespace.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=73)** I talk about why we use codespaces earlier in this course, so for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=82)** I'll go to the src folder, open the app folder, services and click on message.service.ts to open the file.

>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=103)** Use the signal function to create a new reactive value.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=107)** We do this here on line 11 and assign it to the public property called unread.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=113)** This function takes one required argument, the initial value for your signal.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=118)** You can pass in any value you need and the signal function will do its best to set the correct types for your data.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=124)** You can also set the types manually like we did here if you need to.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=128)** The signal function returns something called a writeable signal, which gives you helper methods to modify the data inside your signal.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=136)** Use the set method to replace the existing value with new data.

> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=147)** Use the update method like we do on line 34 to return a new value based on the current data.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=154)** Here we take the current value in the signal and return all messages except the one selected for deletion.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=161)** You can use a method called mutate to change the current value directly instead of returning a new value.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=167)** Let me show you what that might look like.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=169)** Instead of calling the update method, I would call this.unread.mutate which gives me access to the value in the callback function.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=180)** Instead of returning a new array of messages, I might use the splice method to modify the data in place and remove only the selected item.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-signal-function?u=76281980&t=188)** Something like this.

>

#### Angular signals and the Computed function
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Starting in version 16 the Angular team introduced something called signals that you can use to make your code more reactive.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=7)** Earlier in this course we looked at Angular signal function and how to use it to make new reactive values.

>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=52)** Use the computed function to create a new reactive value based on existing signals.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=57)** We do this here on line 13 and assign it to the public property called count.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=64)** This function takes a callback that returns a value that uses data from one or more other signals.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=71)** Here on line 14, we access the value of the unread property by calling it like a function.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=79)** Calling the signal gives us access to all the methods available to the underlying value type.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=84)** In our case here, we access the length property on the array of messages and return its value.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=90)** Here's three things to keep in mind when using computed signals.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=94)** First, computed signals are read only.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=97)** You access their value by calling it like a function, but you can't modify the value directly.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=102)** Computed signals update automatically whenever one or more of the underlying signals change.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=108)** Second, Angular will cache the value of a computed signal once calculated.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=113)** This makes them great for things like filtering a large array of items or doing complex math.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=118)** And finally, Angular uses lazy evaluation to calculate computed signals and the underlying signals they rely on.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=126)** This means Angular will check the value of a signal only when called.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=130)** This makes them very performant and keeps your application fast.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=135)** As of this, recording, signals are in developer preview, which means the API may change at any time based on feedback from the community.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=143)** Check out Angular's official documentation for the most up-to-date info on signals and how to use them.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=149)** Take a moment and think about where you might be able to use signals in your project.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-computed-function?u=76281980&t=153)** Any place your data changes over time might be a good candidate for a signal.

#### Angular signals and the Effect function

>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-effect-function?u=76281980&t=95)** This makes them very performant, and keeps your application fast.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-effect-function?u=76281980&t=98)** They also always execute asynchronously, during a change detection cycle.

> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-effect-function?u=76281980&t=102)** This means you should not rely on them, to update the UI or do blocking tasks.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-effect-function?u=76281980&t=108)** Effects run at least once and then run again, whenever any of the underlying signals change.

> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-signals-and-the-effect-function?u=76281980&t=117)** The easiest way to do this, is to set up your effects, inside the constructor of your components, services, and directives like we did here.
