#### Angular's async pipe
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The async pipe is a powerful tool that helps you work with promises and observables, directly inside your Angular templates.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=6)** All Angular developers should know how to use the async pipe.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=10)** As of this recording, Angular includes the async pipe as a standard built-in tool that comes with the framework.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=16)** This means you can use it in any template right out of the box, no extra code required.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=21)** I opened our project here in a GitHub codespace.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=24)** I talk about why we use codespaces earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=27)** For now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=33)** I'll go to the Source folder, open up the App folder, Pages, Players, and click on players.component.html to open the file.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=43)** This is the Angular template for our main players page.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=47)** The async pipe works in most places where you want to access the data from inside a promise or observable.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=53)** I do that here in the ngIf directive on line six.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=59)** We looked at Angular directives in detail earlier in this course.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=62)** For now, just know that it's a way to attach dynamic behavior to elements inside your Angular templates.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=68)** I referenced the players$ property that comes from our page controller, and pass it into the async pipe using this vertical bar, which is sometimes called a pipe symbol, hence the name.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=80)** As a side note, putting the dollar sign at the end of the property name, is a standard convention when working with observables in Angular.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=87)** It's not required, but I like using it because it makes it clear that the property is an observable, and I'll need to unwrap it to get to the data.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=95)** Behind the scenes, the async pipe manages all the setup and change detection internally, so you don't have to.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=101)** This means less code in your controllers.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=103)** The pipe also unsubscribes from observables automatically, when you destroy the component.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=108)** This helps optimize your code by preventing memory leaks and duplicate subscriptions.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=114)** I'm using an optional syntax called async as, which you can see right here.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=119)** I use the as keyword to assign the return value from the async pipe to a local variable called players, here in the template.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=128)** I can then reference this local variable in other places inside this down element in all its children.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=134)** You don't have to use the async as syntax, but I like to use it because it means I can reference the async pipe only once, and use the local variable everywhere else.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=144)** Without the local variable, I would need to use the async pipe every time I reference the promise or observable from the controller.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=152)** With either syntax, the async pipe returns the data and gives you access to all the methods available to the underlying value type.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=159)** In our case here, we pass the local players variable into the ngFor directive on line seven, which gives us access to each item in the players array.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=170)** We looked at the ngFor directive in detail earlier in this course.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=174)** Take a moment, and think about where you might be able to use the async pipe in your project.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=179)** Any place where you get data from inside a promise or observable, might be a good candidate for the async pipe.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/angular-s-async-pipe?u=76281980&t=185)** Check out the exercise files for links to more info.

#### RxJS and Reactive programming in Angular
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Reactive programming is a software design pattern for working with data that may change over time.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=6)** It's not specific to the Angular framework, but it is a core concept and every Angular developer should know how it works.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=13)** I opened our project here in a GitHub code space.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=16)** I talk about why we use code spaces earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=18)** So for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=25)** I'll go to the source folder, open the app folder, services, and click on api.service.ts to open the file.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=34)** This is the code for our custom API service.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=36)** We looked at writing services in detail earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=39)** So for this video, we'll focus on just the things that relate to reactive programming.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=44)** Our custom service needs to make network requests to get all the player data from our database.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=50)** We use Angular's built-in HTTP client service to make those requests.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=56)** We inject the HTTP service on line 12 as a private class member and reference the instance on line 16 to make a get request to get our data.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=65)** The HTTP service gives us access to all the REST methods we need to work with a database, including this get method.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=74)** Get takes two arguments.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=76)** The first is a string representing the URL for the data.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=79)** This can be any valid URL including a local URL like we have here.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=88)** The second argument is an optional object of request settings.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=91)** We don't have any custom settings for this request so we don't need it here.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=96)** While not required, it's also a good idea to set the return type of the get method like this.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=102)** Get will use a generic object if you don't set the return value.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=108)** The get method returns an observable, which gives us access to all the tools available from RxJS.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=114)** One of those tools is the pipe method.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=119)** The pipe method you see here lets you transform data inside an observable by composing functions together.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=126)** These functions are called operators.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=128)** The output of each operator becomes the input of the next.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=132)** Here, we pipe the response from our get request into a function called delay.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=136)** We're using delay here to simulate network lag so we can see the loading states in our app, but you can use the pipe method to do all kinds of transformations.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=146)** On line 20, we call the getAllPlayers method and use the pipe again to transform the data using the RxJS map operator.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=155)** The map function takes a callback that gives us access to the data inside the observable.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=161)** We then iterate over each item in the list of players and return the first player that matches the ID.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=168)** We do something similar on line 27 where we pipe the return value from getAllPlayers into the RxJS map function, this time iterating over each item and filtering the list to include only the players that match by the name property.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=186)** RxJS has its own filter operator, but we can't use it here because it would operate on the array of players as a whole instead of each individual item in the array.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=196)** Keep in mind that RxJS comes with hundreds of operators and dozens of coding patterns to help you write reactive code.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=203)** I could record an entire course on using RxJS and this video only scratches the surface of what you can do.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/rxjs-and-reactive-programming-in-angular?u=76281980&t=209)** Check out the exercise files for links to more info.

### 7. Testing and Debugging

#### Overview of unit testing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-unit-testing?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-unit-testing?u=76281980&t=0)** - Unit testing is a way to make sure the code you write works the way you expect it to.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/overview-of-unit-testing?u=76281980&t=5)** Every angular developer should know how to test code.
>

>

#### Unit testing without dependency injection
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Earlier in this course, we looked at what unit testing is and how you would run an existing suite of unit tests in your project.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=7)** In this video, we'll keep things simple and look at one common pattern for unit testing angular pipes.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=13)** I opened our project here in a GitHub code space.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=16)** I talk about why we use code spaces earlier in this course, so for now, just think of them as an easy way to share and write code without installing anything on your local machine.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=24)** I'll go to the source folder, open the app folder, pipes, join, and click on join.pipe.spec.ts to open the file.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=35)** Always use the spec.ts ending for your unit tests.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=39)** The Angular CLI tool does this for you when you generate new files using the NG generate command.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=45)** This file has all the unit tests for our custom join pipe.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=50)** Pipes are pure stateless functions by default.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=52)** This means they always return the same output for a given set of inputs.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=56)** This makes them easier to test when compared to services and components.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=60)** That said, you can use this pattern for any code that doesn't use Angular's dependency injection.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=66)** I talk about dependency injection in detail earlier in this course.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=69)** For now, just know that it's a software design pattern and a core concept in the Angular framework.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=75)** In Angular, all unit tests start with a describe block like the one you see here on line three.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=82)** The described function comes from Jasmine, which is the testing framework that comes with Angular.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=87)** The function lets us group individual tests called specs into a collection, sometimes called the suite.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=94)** The first argument of the described function is a string that identifies the suite in our test output.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=100)** You can use any string you'd like but it's common to use the name of the thing you want to test.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=104)** In our case here, we're testing the joint pipe, so we can just use the class name as the description like you see here.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=112)** The second argument is a callback function that holds all the code related to this suite of tests.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=118)** Jasmine comes with many built-in functions for writing and running test code, but the two you'll use most often in a test suite are before each and it.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=128)** We use the before each function on line six.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=131)** The function takes a callback, which Jasmine runs once before every test in the suite.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=136)** In our case, the only thing we need to do is create a new instance of the pipe class.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=141)** Notice that I reassigned an existing variable instead of declaring a new one.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=146)** It's common to declare reusable variables near the top of the suite like we do here on line four.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=153)** These are usually the components, services, and other utilities you use across all your tests.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=159)** Jasmine follows standard JavaScript scope rules so variables declared in an outer scope are always available in interscope.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=169)** That's why I use the let keyword instead of const.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=172)** I like to use immutable data whenever I can but in this case, I want to create a new instance of the pipe each time I run a test.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=179)** While not required, it's good practice and guarantees a clean copy of the pipe each time.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=184)** We use the it function like you see on lines 10 and 17 to declare the actual test logic as well as any test specific setup for the code.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=194)** The function takes two arguments.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=196)** The first is a string to label the test.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=199)** In my work, we usually start the label with the word should, as in should do something or should return something or should not do something, but you can use any label that works well for you and your team.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=212)** The second argument is a callback.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=214)** This is where we put all our test logic and setup code.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=218)** Frameworks like Jasmine use something called assertions to do the testing.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=222)** This is the part of the test code where you compare the actual result against an expectation.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=228)** You see the assertions on lines 14 and 21 here in our tests.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=235)** The expect function comes from Jasmine.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=239)** We pass in the actual result as the first argument of this function and change something called a matcher to define our expectations.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=247)** Jasmine comes with many built-in matchers, most of which take an expected result as the first argument.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=252)** This toBe matcher is very common.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=256)** It checks the expected result against the actual result using strict JavaScript equality.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=262)** So what Line 14 really means is we expect the actual result to be strictly equal to the expected result.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=271)** The test passes if the assertion is true, it fails if the assertion is false.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=276)** I like to assign the expected and actual results to named variables, but it's not required.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=281)** You could pass the values directly into the Jasmine functions like this and the test would be just as valid.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=290)** Testing and debugging is a huge part of writing Angular apps.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/unit-testing-without-dependency-injection?u=76281980&t=294)** Be sure to check out my other course on testing and debugging Angular applications for a detailed look at all the things you can do with Angular's testing tools.

#### Static code linting in Angular
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Code linting, sometimes called static code analysis, is a type of automated test that checks your code for errors without compiling or running the code.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=10)** Think of it like a grammar guide for your project.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=12)** It can't tell you if the code will run, but it can tell you if you put the curly brackets in the correct place.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=19)** The biggest benefit of code linting is consistency between developers.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=23)** We all have a personal style, and linting the code is a great way to make sure everyone on your team writes code in the same way.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=29)** This makes the code easier to read, easier to search, and more predictable.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=35)** I opened our project here in a GitHub code space.
>

>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=46)** I'll go to the root of our project and click on .eslintrc.json.

> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=60)** As of this recording, it has an active community with over 900 contributors on GitHub and almost 27 million weekly downloads on NPM.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=69)** It's not part of the default install for new Angular projects, but you can quickly add it by running the NG lint or NPM run lint commands from your terminal.

> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=137)** This time I get an error message that includes the file path, the exact line and starting character, and details about which linting rules triggered the error.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=149)** I'll need to fix the code to get rid of the error.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=151)** To do that, I'll revert my change and run the linter again.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=157)** Boom, this time, no errors.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=159)** There are lots of configuration options you can use to customize Es lint to suit your project.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/angular-essential-training-22884621/static-code-linting-in-angular?u=76281980&t=164)** Be sure to check out the exercise files for links to more info.
